1439156816|2.0|One piece of advice Stephen King offers in this, his 2000 rumination about the fiction writer's craft as he experienced it, is that a writer can't be afraid to chuck a draft after a couple hundred pages and start again if it's not working. \\"[E]ven when it breaks your egocentric little scribbler's heart, kill your darlings.\\"<br /><br />I can't help but think he should have followed his own advice here.<br /><br />During the summer of 1999, in the middle of writing this book, King was run over by a Dodge van. For the next few months, he lived in constant pain, his right leg below the knee described as being like \\"marbles in a sock.\\" With his wife Tabitha's help, he found the will to return to his writing desk, to write this very book in fact. With the experience of writing came the kind of joy and renewed sense of purpose that drove him to endure and prevail.<br /><br />All this would have made a great starting point for a memoir/rumination on the nature of writing, except for the fact King was already well into this book when the accident happened, a book that starts with a lengthy-enough memoir about his discovery and pursuit of fiction-writing in his youth. To chuck all that would mean losing a lot of work, at a time when just sitting down was painful. What is billed on the cover and title as a kind of \\"how-to\\" book becomes instead a short peek behind the wizard's curtain sandwiched by two large pieces of autobiography that use writing more as a lens for King viewing his life before and after he was hit.<br /><br />King makes the point more than once that he likes to write without an outline, with no idea where the plot will go. This obviously works for him but is probably bad advice to the layman. I offer this book as Exhibit A in that regard.<br /><br />When he gets around to talking about writing itself, he offers some good notes (\\"I believe the road to hell is paved with adverbs\\") but it doesn't really get to the heart of anything. Most writers won't be able to take their writing to the next level anyway, he grudgingly admits, as what God gives you to work with only can only be raised so high. Since most geniuses are crazy and miserable company, you are probably better off, he offers as consolation, but I don't think buyers of the book were hungry for insights like this.<br /><br />Another signature moment: King says his ideas come to him like fossils buried in the ground, that when a reporter expressed skepticism King told him it didn't matter if he didn't believe him, as long as King believed it. As a reader, I find that a fascinating insight as to how the man works. As a writer, I have no idea what he's talking about.<br /><br />For King's legion of fans, his \\"constant readers,\\" there will no doubt be much to enjoy in King's tales of youthful story rejection (he posted the letters on a nail in his bedroom and treasured the ones which came with a brief personal note of encouragement) and how he experienced the writing of some of his best-known books. \\"The Shining,\\" for example, turned out to have a hidden message directed at the author himself, which he talks about with refreshing candor.<br /><br />\\"On Writing\\" is a pleasant time-passer with some bits of real interest scattered about, but King never seems to find his focus. With many if not most of King's other books, you don't have to be a fan of his going in to enjoy them. Not so here.take their writing to the next level anyway, he grudgingly admits, as what God gives you to work with only can only be raised so high. Since most geniuses are crazy and miserable company, you are probably better off, he offers as consolation, but I don't think buyers of the book were hungry for insights like this.<br /><br />Another signature moment: King says his ideas come to him like fossils buried in the ground, that when a reporter expressed skepticism King told him it didn't matter if he didn't believe him, as long as King believed it. As a writer, I have no idea what he's talking about.<br /><br />For King's legion of fans, his \\"constant readers,\\" there will no doubt be much to enjoy in King's tales of youthful story rejection (he posted the letters on a nail in his bedroom and treasured the ones which came with a brief personal note of encouragement) and how he experienced the writing of some of his best-known books. \\"The Shining,\\" for example, turned out to have a hidden message directed at the author himself, which he talks about with refreshing candor.<br /><br />\\"On Writing\\" is a pleasant time-passer with some bits of real interest scattered about, but King never seems to find his focus. With many if not most of King's other books, you don't have to be a fan of his going in to enjoy them. Not so here.

B00729GQ0A|3.0|I received this book when the author did a Twitter giveaway contest (which I thought was awesome, it was Hangman!). I was excited to add it to my long \\"To Be Read\\" list. When I finally got to it, I was disappointed with the first 70% of the book. The main character, Sophia, is supposed to be a 22-year-old college graduate, but she acts much more like a 16-year-old girl. It was beyond annoying. The story is written in the first person, so we have the pleasure of copious amounts of \\"telling, not showing.\\" Sophia is attracted to a supernatural man, Charles, which is evident in her actions, but because the reader knows her thoughts, we're beaten over the head with her feelings. How many times can a person tell us about the \\"heat\\" or \\"warmth\\" they feel when someone touches them? I wish this was the only thing that was repetitive, but Sophia feels the need to really make sure we understand every single emotion she's feeling by way of telling us over and over. And when she's not doing that, she telling us exactly what she/other characters are wearing, because that somehow matters.<br /><br />I will say that the last 30% of the book was very well written. The only issue I had was that Sophia's gift to read others' thoughts robbed the reader of \\"seeing\\" how other characters were feeling/reacting because Sophia would just tell us. But for the most part, all of the loose ends were tied up and the action/battle scenes were rivoting. It's almost like someone else wrote them. Maybe the author matured as she wrote the story, but I wish she would have went back and reworked the beginning. The story deserved it and I SO wanted to like this book. Unfortunately, it's unlikely I'll read the next in the series or anything else by this author, even though I enjoyed the overall story.

0441013597|5.0|I fell into the DUNE universe for about five years back in the 1970's. Since a few years ago I read the Brian Herbert and Kevin J. Anderson prequels and two sequels. Lately I have been revisiting the original six Dune novels. OK, so I have a lot of time on my hands. I am a big fan of long form science fiction. I got into it in high school in the 50's with Isaac Asimov's Foundation Series. DUNE is far more interesting once the reader continues beyond the opening story to follow \\"The Golden Path\\". Some reviewer or other said the story was humorless. What could be more humorless than \\"1984\\" or \\"Fahrenheit 451\\"?  In DUNE the human race just barely escaped more than a thousand years of slavery under the oppression by their own machines. During the following millennia all human institutions had to be remade. I see the situation as similar to the Renaissance after a thousand years of darkness at the fall of Rome. All authority was in the hands of closely held insular institutions and conflict was only resolved by war or alliances of families. Religions and secret societies ruled for another 500 years. In DUNE Chronicles we get a much expanded and detailed version. As a result of the Machine Revolution and the rebuilding the human race had evolved in unexpected directions and then settled into  a violent form of stagnation. In DUNE an unexpected element appears and unhinges all that stagnation and releases pent-up passions. Thus the human  race begins a desperate race against total destruction by unknown forces. Reading Dune is much like reading Moby Dick or War and Peace, much elaborate dialogue and description building to the occasional climax.
B001E45XT4|3.0|First of all, I found this headset to have great sound quality and I really enjoy using it as a gaming headset. However, my gripe is that on some days I need to use the headset to make Skype calls that can last extended periods of time, and I find the headset gets extremely uncomfortable because the way the earpieces are insulated can make it really hot and uncomfortable around the ears. Another small gripe is that the headset is actually huge, and way bigger than I expected. Wearing it for a long time can be uncomfortable for this reason also, because of the weight. If it had been cheaper I probably wouldn't have minded so much, but for the value for price I found these little design flaws disappointing.

How old is this author?  I kept asking a much older woman at work, who told me about these books and raves about them, if the author wrote teen fiction because it doesn't seem like adult fiction at all to me.  When people talk about this book and the other two about being, \\"brilliant\\", it makes me laugh.  There is nothing original about the series.  There are some peaks of interest throughout, maybe more so in book 2, but it's basically the same repetition.  It gets tiring.  The sex scenes are boring, predictable and repetitious.  I am on the 3rd book presently and am skipping through most of it just to see how it ends.  Ana doesn't like how bossy Christian is, how overbearing and overprotective he is, making him take her to work, always having to have her phone on in case he calls.  This woman is not allowed to stick up for herself.  No wonder why she acts like a 15 year old.  On paper, that is how she comes across. Right now, it's almost impossible to write a review without talking about, or at least mentioning, the letter. Read it if you haven't already. It's important. And at the time this review was written, tons of celebrities have already offered their reactions, read: Russell Simmons, Chuck D, Solange Knowles, Stephen Hill, and then, of course Tyler the Creator and Earl Sweatshirt. And many others, to follow, I'm sure.<br /><br />But enough about the letter. There's music to talk about and questions to answer. Like, is this album any good? Does Andr&eacute; 3000 deliver on the track `Pink Matter?' And, what does the John Mayer collaboration track actually sound like? Let's start by answering all of that right now: yes, the album is very good. Yes Andr&eacute; 3000 delivers (and more) on arguably, and probably, one of the better (if not best) tracks of this record. And John Mayer doesn't say anything because `White' is an instrumental track and it sounds like what you would expect: Frank Ocean and John Mayer, in the studio, jamming together. And I say `focused' and `with purpose' because Channel Orange is not littered with guests. Just Earl Sweatshirt, John Mayer (who doesn't even sing or anything, remember that!) and Andr&eacute; 3000. The track titles themselves are pretty remarkable (I think) and full of gumption (`Super Rich Kids,' `Pyramids,' `Pink Matter,' `Forrest Gump,' etc.) and rest assured, it never drags (55 minutes and then some).<br /><br />Album highlights include:<br /><br />Thinkin Bout You<br />Sweet Life<br />Super Rich Kids (feat. Earl Sweatshirt)<br />Pilot Jones<br />Pyramids<br />White (feat. John Mayer)<br />Bad Religion<br />Pink Matter (feat. Andr&eacute; 3000)<br /><br />As a whole, Channel Orange is, quite honestly, probably the definitive R&B album this side of 2012. And this is what 2012 R&B sounds like. B004J4WN0I|3.0|As many reviews have mentioned, this series is clearly derivative of Jim Butcher's Harry Dresden novels. Also, as others have mentioned, Atticus O'Sullivan is no Harry Dresden. I've read Hounded, Hexed and Hammered, and am in the midst of Tricked. I find myself frustrated and mildly disappointed. Hearne has sufficient talent as a writer to have kept me going for these four books, but his flaws are just as persistent as his strengths.<br /><br />At age 48, I have acquired a certain perspective that I could not even have imagined at age 21. Things that seemed so important then seem trivial now. From his author pictures on his web site, I'd guess Hearne himself to be maybe late 30s (early 40s? kind of hard to tell) -- old enough to have a bit of perspective (then again, I've learned a lot about life since my late 30s), but I guess not everyone reaches the same points in their life journeys at the same time.<br /><br />I was recently mentioning to someone in regard to another novel I'd read that it is very difficult to write a character who is wiser and more mature than you are. 2100 years is a crushing burden of life experience, a crucible that would leave no man unscathed. O'Sullivan seems to have absorbed little of it. His moral and political viewpoints are those of the arrogant 21-year-old hipster that O'Sullivan \\"pretends\\" to be, not those of someone who has witnessed centuries of human folly. Harry Dresden, by contrast, has enormous physical and mental scars and baggage from his short life beneath the pen of Jim Butcher. Compelling heroes and heroines are flawed, they suffer, and their wisdom, insights and victories are hard-won. O'Sullivan has precipitated world-altering events literally by accident, and so far seems remarkably unaffected.<br /><br />One of the things I find most frustrating is that Hearne is constantly sucker-punching conservative readers like myself with his political/social opinions. I have absolutely no idea what Jim Butcher's or Patricia Briggs' or Kim Harrison's political beliefs are and I like it that way. They're all very respectful of their readers. Hearne's/O'Sullivan's constant snarking on conservatives is profoundly annoying and makes me feel guilty for financially supporting him by buying his books. Does he not realize that there are many fantasy readers out here who are conservatives and that alienating us makes no sense? The attitude I get from the writing is that Hearne/O'Sullivan obviously thinks his leftist viewpoints and great regard for crunchy, hippie greenies is the cool, correct thing and *obviously* the cool people reading his books agree with him. It's hard to respect a supposedly 2,100-year-old Druid whose political and moral philosophy is no more intellectually developed than a 20-something or 30-something ironic hipster.<br /><br />The flippant approach appears in other regards, as well.<br /><br />The deaths of secondary characters pass quickly because they are too shallowly drawn to allow us to care much about them, and O'Sullivan himself seems to grieve little -- about the only thing 2100 years has granted him is a callous indifference to the deaths of others. And, you'd think the death of one god would be pretty much a lifetime achievement, but it turns out that killing gods is like eating potato chips: you start with one and next thing you know you've downed a half dozen. Yes, at once point in Tricked he breaks down and weeps at the enormity of what he's done and how stupid he has been, but it's written in such a brief, perfunctory manner, it's not convincing. So far, there seems to be a distinct lack of consequence considering what he has done.<br /><br />His apprentice Granuaile is superficially appealing, but woefully underdeveloped. She so far serves little purpose beyond chauffeur and attractive cheerleader for O'Sullivan, and that's a shame because she would seem to have great potential, if she could be spared a little limelight. She pales greatly next to the depth ofcharacter that Dresden's friend Karrin Murphy displays (but maybe that's not fair, because I can't say that Murphy's development was as mature after three and a half books), or even compared to Dresden's own apprentice, Molly Carpenter -- Molly has had struggles and character arcs that make her nearly as interesting a character as Dresden himself.<br /><br />I'd *like* to be able to keep enjoying the things I do enjoy about the series: it's fast paced, the dialog is moderately witty, the character of Oberon has developed quite a bit, and we've been given a hint or two that we'll actually get to know the Morrigan as a real, thinking, feeling person, given enough time. Most of all, there's plenty of foreshadowing that all O'Sullivan's many mistakes will catch up with him and he won't be able to keep dodging the consequences. Maybe then things will get real.

But it was too late for me; I had already ordered it, and it was sitting on my shelf awhile unopened.<br />  So I put in the first CD. The sound was loud and distorted; I let it play awhile, until I couldn't stand it anymore. I'm not going to listen to the rest; I'm not going to use better equipment; I've heard enough.<br />  I was wondering if earlier CDs were better. About the earlier 2006 2o-bit box set on amazon, I found this review at the top of the reviews, by Allan K Betz:<br />  \\"...there is a piercing digital distortion in the mid-frequency range. Between about 2000 and 8000 cycles per second a nasty ring comes out and bites your ears off. It so bad that some notes jump right out of the mix as if their volume were twice that of notes in other ranges...\\"<br />  What about Columbia Legacy? To me, those sounded better than the original CDs. I guess I'm learning the hard way to listen to things as soon as I get them, so I can return them if I have to. I'm learning so much from them, and I couldn't learn these things any other way. Even those who do the tedious job of listing what's on the CD and technical info help me. Often, this is the only way I know what's on the CD. Sometimes I'm looking at the pictures with a magnifying glass. Thanks again. I almost forgot: We're all united in our love of music.<br />  P.S. starting from 7\22\'12  -I'm indebted to those who sell their recordings and video used (I especially like \\"like new\\") through amazon: CDs, of my favorite performers, I never saw at Tower Records stores while they were alive (and I thought Tower Records was the best.), CDs, of my favorite records, I never knew existed as CDs; I had given up hope until seeing them on amazon. If I remember, correctly, my favorite audio magazine said about it at the time,\\"...benefits show up in a tight, clear bass, remarkably vivid midrange, and delicate highs...\\" But it froze from years of me not using it-convenient CDs came along. I could hardly bare throwing it away. I wish they would make it again, but add a built-in phono amp.)<br />  Though I'm questioning the sound of this set, I do have CDs of historical recordings. I don't expect them to sound so good, but I'm thrilled when they do. Reminds me of Toscanini. To me, Arturo Toscanini was the most intense conductor of the 20th century. (Sorry he was reputed to be a tyrant.) An author of a book about him said concert-goers leaving after the end of Toscanini's concerts said that was the most beautiful sound they had ever heard. But the author goes on to say words, to the effect, that no matter how beautiful the live sound, if the recorded sound is junk, people are not going to listen.<br />  I hardly listen to Toscanini anymore, except for his Saint-Saens Organ Symphony with the NBC Symphony on RCA. When I hear other performances over the radio, I still think of Toscanini'sin my head. Several years ago, I heard Toscanini's Brahms symphonies for the first time. I thought they were the most intense I had ever heard-and I thought James Levine's First with the CSO on RCA, and Bernard Haitink's Fourth with the Concgb.O. on Universal Classics- were intense. ( Haitink is my favorite conductor, but I have other favorites, too-like Giulini, Arthur Fiedler, Markevitch, Dorati, Reiner, Marriner with the ASMF especially playing Mozart, Krips with Concgb.O playing Mozart, Karel Ancerl with the Czech Philharnonic, Alexander Schneider, Charles Gerhardt, Raymond Leppard, Leonard Slatkin, Jaap Schroder, Harnoncourt- I hardly listen to him anymore, but I was enthralled listening recently to his Schubert's 9th, the \\"Great\\" in C major, playing with the Concgb.O. on Teldec [though here it's called No.8, and the Unfinished called No.7], equal to my favorite, Toscanini's playing with the Philadelphia O. on RCA [but I don't like his NBC account], but of course with better sound -JoAnn Faletta, Andreas Delfs [ I remember a concert, a few years ago, with the now-bankrupt Honolulu Symphony, then America's oldest symphony west of the Rockies-his \\"Blue Danube\\" waltz was moving and at times unexpectedly tender. Tears came down my face. But that was a hard time in my life, too. And they played the same composers' \\"Egyptian March\\" -with some of the orchestra members singing.], now I'm discovering Robert Craft with Stravinsky, discovering Christoph von Dohnanyi with the Cleveland O. playing Dvorak, and others....) But try and hear Toscanini's on RCA with the UV 22 Super CD Encoding, if you wish.<br />  But the problem with the Sony Mozart\Perahia sets seem to be the remastering. Maybe Sony should let outsiders-music-loving listeners and musicians-all with good hearing-listen to the CDs before mass-production.<br />  I was re-reading the other reviews. It's like night and day. It's like I'm not even listening to the same product. Is it possible there's manufacturing differences in quality of this product-like about wine, when the same bottles of a wine don't taste all the same, called \\"bottle variation\\"? I don't know.<br />  It's a given it's not likely we're listening through the same equipment. But the exact equipment reviewers use should not become an issue, if the equipment sounds OK to the reviewer.<br />  I don't want to say the obvious: you can buy the earlier, individual CDs, or buy another performer's boxed set. If you must know, I'm a big fan of Mitsuko Uchida, from the first notes I heard her playing a Mozart piano sonata on a CD.<br />  I haven't heard everything she plays; but of the many pieces I heard, there's some I don't care for. Recently, I bought her earlier Mozart piano concertos set used(I was missing individual CDs)- instead of the most recent set-after a reviewer wrote of the later set-the earliest piano concertos aren't performed by her, but by someone else( Ingrid Haebler? I'm not sure.) Also, that set may have concertos split between two CDs. But please read the reviews-good and bad-of both sets, or of whomever performers you are interested in. I'm listening right now to the Uchida set for the first time. I put in the first CD; it sounds fine... I'm in heaven. But I'm not sure if I'm hearing the earliest concertos. I haven't heard those before; and the set doesn't name them by No., but by KV No. Let me figure this out: this set has a booklet; in the Italian-language section, they're listed by No. and K.No. It looks like concertos 1-4,7, are not here. But, wait, No.10 K.365 is listed in the Italian section, but not on the CD list in the front of the booklet; so No.10 is not here, too. This is a 9-CD set compiled in 1993; and none of the concertos are split between two CDs. I find out, later, I'm mistaken: Ingrid Haebler plays the first four concertos with the Capella Academica Wien conducted by Eduard Melkus on Alfred Brendel's 12-CD set, Collector's Edition, 2011 on Decca. But these performances of the first four concertos are boring, and I expected better sound for something recorded in 1973. (I also bought this Brendel set about the same time I got the other two sets.) A reviewer wrote, something like, these first four concertos are not the best compositions-you won't miss them. I could agree with that; but I remember times in the past, I thought I didn't like a composition, untill the right performer, performances brought it alive, or made the most musical sense out of it-(which is one reason why I like Haitink so much- his thoughtfullness brings out the intensity of a piece, but not wring out all the emotions of a piece and not play fast movements too fast, and slower movements too slow. But I don't like every performance he conducts.<br />  To me, the most important thing a conductor can do is choose the right tempos. But what happens when the tempo sounds wrong? To me, musicians should have the authority, by secret vote, to override the conductor. If the conductor can't first convince the orchestra of his or her ideas, how is he or she going to convince the listener?)<br />  Several months ago, I heard, for the first time, a CD by Brendel-Mozart piano concertos Nos. 9, 25, and part of 22., the first CD in a Phillps Duo. I let that CD play over and over again. When I finally stopped, I didn't want to listen to any other sounds; I didn't want anything to distract me from hearing that music leftover in my head...<br />  Both Uchida sets are missing the same concertos.<br />  I bought the Perahia set to hear for myself why two Guides, whose names I'm figuring out I'm not supposed to name on amazon, gave the Perahia set their most highest rating, but not to Uchida's, even though they gave their most highest rating to her Mozart piano sonatas, with another solo piano piece, the Fantasia in C Minor, KV475, my favorite piece on there. My first Mozart piano concerto CD of hers I got, was No.20 KV466 with No.21 KV467(Elvira Madigan). It was so good, I had to get the rest by her., what I could get. I remember playing the \\"Elvira Madigan\\" concerto first, and playing the famous Andante over and over again. Trying to hear for myself, why one of the Guides has, about Uchida's,\\"...individually none of the peformances of the major works here would be a first choice...\\" I've disagreed with the Guides before.<br />  My copies of the Guides are a few years old, so I don't know what's changed since then. But as I'm finding out, I'd rather depend on the reviews on amazon.<br />  If you want to know further-dare I say it?-from the some music I listened to, Perahia's Mozart sounds like... Chopin. But a reviewer wrote Uchida's Beethoven sounds like Mozart.<br />  No one alive now has heard Mozart playing his music on the forte-pianos of his time. Or if Mozart were alive today, after hearing modern instruments and music of the world, how he would want his music to sound. We choose the performances that sing to us( or does it choose us, like how a cat chooses its owner?) within the known wishes of the composer, and other considerations. (Again, I think of Toscanini telling his musicians,\\"Cantare, cantare![sing]\\", but never explain to them what he meant. And Toscanini did not always follow the composer's instructions. I remember reading about a rehearsal of one of Ravel's pieces, Ravel told Toscanini that's not how he(Ravel) wanted it played. I don't know if Toscanini followed.)<br /><br />  If it seems I've wandered off the subject at times, I'm trying to show-I'm a first-time reviewer whose likes in music you didn't know; there's a whole world of music out there-you won't miss this one Sony set; and I'm human -I'm not a sound monster attacking something sacred. Like fairy tales? Imagine the Emperors' New Clothes...<br />  I feel stupid. I found out too late: I discovered when I click on the product, the amazon page shows up with the product details-like what's on the CD, reviews, write a review, sometimes listen to samples (though I don't know how to do this yet) and other info. Before, I was clicking on the reviews(the stars), then clicking \\"used\\" or \\"new\\", which didn't take me to the amazon product page. But many of you probably knew this already, it's me who didn't know. So, please click on the product to get on to the amazon product page, besides reading the reviews. I only discovered amazon in April.<br />  Oh yes, I just lowered my rating to 1 star, because it's unlikely I'll ever hear the rest of this set. And the enlivening Hawaii Symphony Ochestra, made up mostly of musicians of the past symphony, played its first season in April and May, to mostly standing ovations. Under better management-even the musicians say so(and I'm pro-union)-this is a story better told by someone more qualified than me. We here had to learn the hard way: support your symphony. And as my doctor said about this, \\"When something is gone, that's when you miss it.\\"<br />  I'm worried about the next symphony season in the fall. The concert hall is going to be used for a musical. To me, the simplest-sounding solution is to schedule the symphony season before or after the run of the musical, but I'm definitely not an expert.<br />  Thanks, again.e first four concertos are boring, and I expected better sound for something recorded in 1973. B0015DROBO|3.0|I was warned that it started slow but to keep plugging.  I followed that advice and was ultimately satisfied.  But somewhere during the read -- I'm not exactly sure where -- I found myself asking, \\"How much damn coffee do these people drink?\\"  I might be exagerating a bit, but it seemed like someone was drinking a cup of coffee on every page. Generally I thought the descriptions were fine, but there were those that left me weary, and others that had me thinking, \\"Why did you tell me that?\\"  I can think of one instance in particular, although I don't have the book in front of me to quote it.  But it went something like this:  He went out and wiped down the garden furniture and sat and drank his coffee.  He was wearing a white shirt with the collar turned up.<br /><br />So I get that he was sitting there with his coffee thinking about the case.  But why the hell did you tell me about the shirt he was wearing, especially the detail about the collar.  At first, I thought it might be an important detail.  That it would save his life, that a bullet would ricochet off the neatly starched collar, or something like that.  But no.  Unless I missed something, it seemed like a pointless detail for which most students of creative writing would be vigorously criticized by their professors.<br /><br />Don't get me wrong.  I enjoyed the book. Could hardly put it down.  But I was just a bit perplexed and sometimes distracted by the writing.  Maybe it was just a translation thing.
0375703861|2.0|I don't know how Zadie Smith does it. How it is possible that her novel White Teeth is absolutely hilarious and a complete drag at the same time? I have no idea how she accomplished that, but it's quite an extraordinary feat.<br /><br />The novel opens in England on New Year's Day of 1975 with the suicide attempt of Archie Jones. But before you prejudge that scenario to be grim and depressing, the man who finds Archie and saves him proclaims \\"We're not licensed for suicides around here. This place halal. Kosher, understand? If you're going to die round here, my friend, I'm afraid you've got to be thoroughly bled first.\\" The comedic effect is unmistakable, and it continues throughout the novel. Next we learn about the story of Archie's friend, Samad Iqbal, and then one by one, we learn about their respective family members.<br /><br />Using her novel as the vehicle to critique society, Smith tries to tackle all of the following issues: class, religion, beauty, race, God, immigration, uncertainty, war, the past, generational gap, assimilation, extremist groups, family, history, colonization, science versus faith, and relationships. All of those issues? Yes, and maybe even more. Overly ambitious? I think so. Smith makes a social commentary on all of these issues, pokes fun at them, and draws out the absurd in them. It is funny. Seriously laugh out loud funny.<br /><br />And yet, the story is a drag to read. I'm not sure why. Maybe it's just me. Is it because I don't like comedy in my novels? Perhaps comedy loves company much more than misery does. The best part about laughing is sharing that laugh with others. Comedies are best on TV or in the movies, in a format that allows you to share that laugh with friends. Not as much fun to chuckle to yourself all alone.<br /><br />Well, that's one theory anyway. Another theory is that the author is too ambitious and by trying to cover everything, she ends up covering nothing. There are too many themes to chew over. Too much all at once.<br /><br />Or maybe I didn't like it because as soon as I started to get interested in one character and one storyline, the author switched to another character with another storyline. In the end, she weaves them all together, but by then I was just struggling to finish the book and didn't care anymore. I was not invested in any of the characters. I had nothing and no one to root for as the novel progressed. Oddly, I could see that the ending was supposed to be gathering steam for the final culmination of the book, but I found myself not caring. I just wanted to be done with the darned thing at that point.<br /><br />Undoubtedly, Smith is an intelligent writer. The themes are thick and layered. I'm sure that a literary scholar would be delighted to parse through all the depth she offers. But for the average reader, I'm afraid the story is too much of a drag and getting through the novel just feels like a chore punctuated with random outbursts of laughter.at once.<br /><br />Or maybe I didn't like it because as soon as I started to get interested in one character and one storyline, the author switched to another character with another storyline. In the end, she weaves them all together, but by then I was just struggling to finish the book and didn't care anymore. I was not invested in any of the characters. I had nothing and no one to root for as the novel progressed. Oddly, I could see that the ending was supposed to be gathering steam for the final culmination of the book, but I found myself not caring. I just wanted to be done with the darned thing at that point.<br /><br />Undoubtedly, Smith is an intelligent writer. The themes are thick and layered. I'm sure that a literary scholar would be delighted to parse through all the depth she offers. But for the average reader, I'm afraid the story is too much of a drag and getting through the novel just feels like a chore punctuated with random outbursts of laughter.
